A Little Princess

by

Frances Hodgson Burnett

A LITTLE PRINCESS

Summary: Sara Crewe, a pupil at Miss Minchin's London school, is left in poverty when her (Crewe) father dies, but is later rescued by a mysterious benefactor.

Sara

Once on a dark winter's day, when the yellow fog hung so thick and heavy in the streets of London that the lamps were lighted and the shop windows blazed with gas as they do at night, an odd-looking little girl sat in a cab with her (girl) father and was driven rather slowly through the big thoroughfares.

She (girl) sat with her (girl) feet tucked under her (girl), and leaned against her (girl) father, who held her (girl) in his (father) arm, as she (girl) stared out of the window at the passing people with a queer old-fashioned thoughtfulness in her (girl) big eyes.

She (girl) was such a little girl that one did not expect to see such a look on her (girl) small face. It would have been an old look for a child of twelve, and Sara Crewe was only seven. The fact was, however, that she (Crewe) was always dreaming and thinking odd things and could not herself remember any time when she (Crewe) had not been thinking things about grown-up people and the world they belonged to. She (Crewe) felt as if she (Crewe) had lived a long, long time.

At this moment she (Crewe) was remembering the voyage she (Crewe) had just made from Bombay with her (Crewe) father, Captain Crewe. She (Crewe) was thinking of the big ship, of the Lascars passing silently to and fro on it, of the children playing about on the hot deck, and of some young officers' wives who used to try to make her (Crewe) talk to them and laugh at the things she (Crewe) said.

Principally, she (Crewe) was thinking of what a queer thing it was that at one time one was in India in the blazing sun, and then in the middle of the ocean, and then driving in a strange vehicle through strange streets where the day was as dark as the night. She (Crewe) found this so puzzling that she (Crewe) moved closer to her (Crewe) father.

``papa.'' (she)

She (Sara) did not know all that being rich meant. She (Sara) had always lived in a beautiful bungalow, and had been used to seeing many servants who made salaams to her (Sara) and called her (Sara)
``Missee Sahib,'' (servants)

During her (Sara) short life only one thing had troubled her (Sara), and that thing was

``the place'' (her)
she (Sara) was to be taken to some day. The climate of India was very bad for children, and as soon as possible they were sent away from it--generally to England and to school. She (Sara) had seen other children go away, and had heard their fathers and mothers talk about the letters they received from them. She (Sara) had known that she (Sara) would be obliged to go also, and though sometimes her (Sara) father's stories of the voyage and the new country had attracted her (Sara), she (Sara) had been troubled by the thought that he (father) could not stay with her (Sara).

She (Sara) had liked to think of that. To keep the house for her (Sara) father; to ride with him (father), and sit at the head of his (father) table when he (father) had dinner parties; to talk to him (father) and read his (father) books--that would be what she (Sara) would like most in the world, and if one must go away to

It was a big, dull, brick house, exactly like all the others in its row, but that on the front door there shone a brass plate on which was engraved in black letters:

MISS MINCHIN,

Select Seminary for Young Ladies.

Sara often thought afterward that the house was somehow exactly like Miss Minchin. In the hall everything was hard and polished--even the red cheeks of the moon face on the tall clock in the corner had a severe varnished look. The drawing room into which they were ushered was covered by a carpet with a square pattern upon it, the chairs were square, and a heavy marble timepiece stood upon the heavy marble mantel.

A French Lesson

When Sara entered the schoolroom the next morning everybody looked at her (Sara) with wide, interested eyes. By that time every pupil--from Lavinia Herbert, who was nearly thirteen and felt quite grown up, to Lottie Legh, who was only just four and the baby of the school--had heard a great deal about her (Legh). They knew very certainly that she (Legh) was Miss Minchin's show pupil and was considered a credit to the establishment. 4

Lottie

If Sara had been a different kind of child, the life she (Sara) led at Miss Minchin's Select Seminary for the next few years would not have been at all good for her (Sara). She (Sara) was treated more as if she (Sara) were a distinguished guest at the establishment than as if she (Sara) were a mere little girl. If she (Sara) had been a self-opinionated, domineering child, she (Sara) might have become disagreeable enough to be unbearable through being so much indulged and flattered. If she (Sara) had been an indolent child, she (Sara) would have learned nothing. She (Minchin) knew quite well that if Sara wrote to her (Sara) papa to tell him (papa) she (Sara) was uncomfortable or unhappy, Captain Crewe would remove her (Crewe) at once. Miss Minchin's opinion was that if a child were continually praised and never forbidden to do what she (Crewe) liked, she (Crewe) would be sure to be fond of the place where she (Crewe) was so treated. Accordingly, Sara was praised for her (Sara) quickness at her (Sara) lessons, for her (Sara) good manners, for her (Sara) amiability to her (Sara) fellow pupils, for her (Sara) generosity if she (Sara) gave sixpence to a beggar out of her (Sara) full little purse; the simplest thing she (Sara) did was treated as if it were a virtue, and if she (Sara) had not had a disposition and a clever little brain, she (Sara) might have been a very self-satisfied young person. she (Sara) used to say.

Perhaps I have not really a good temper at all, but if you have everything you want and everyone is kind to you, how can you help but be good-tempered? I don't know'' (unknown)

Becky

Of course the greatest power Sara possessed and the one which gained her (Sara) even more followers than her (Sara) luxuries and the fact that she (Sara) was

``the show pupil,'' (she)

Anyone who has been at school with a teller of stories knows what the wonder means--how he (teller) or she (Lavinia) is followed about and besought in a whisper to relate romances; how groups gather round and hang on the outskirts of the favored party in the hope of being allowed to join in and listen. Sara not only could tell stories, but she (Sara) adored telling them. The Diamond Mines

The birthday was to be celebrated by great festivities. The schoolroom was to be decorated, and there was to be a party. The schoolroom was being decked with garlands of holly; the desks had been moved away, and red covers had been put on the forms which were arrayed round the room against the wall.

In the Attic

The first night she (Becky) spent in her (Becky) attic was a thing Sara never forgot. The Indian Gentleman

But it was a perilous thing for Ermengarde and Lottie to make pilgrimages to the attic. They could never be quite sure when Sara would be there, and they could scarcely ever be certain that Miss Amelia would not make a tour of inspection through the bedrooms after the pupils were supposed to be asleep. She (Sara) had no one to talk to; and when she (Sara) was sent out on errands and walked through the streets, a forlorn little figure carrying a basket or a parcel, trying to hold her (Sara) hat on when the wind was blowing, and feeling the water soak through her (Sara) shoes when it was raining, she (Sara) felt as if the crowds hurrying past her (Sara) made her (Sara) loneliness greater. When she (Sara) had been the Princess Sara, driving through the streets in her (Sara) brougham, or walking, attended by Mariette, the sight of her (Sara) bright, eager little face and picturesque coats and hats had often caused people to look after her (Sara). A happy, beautifully cared for little girl naturally attracts attention. Shabby, poorly dressed children are not rare enough and pretty enough to make people turn around to look at them and smile. No one looked at Sara in these days, and no one seemed to see her (Sara) as she (Sara) hurried along the crowded pavements. She (Sara) had begun to grow very fast, and, as she (Sara) was dressed only in such clothes as the plainer remnants of her (Sara) wardrobe would supply, she (Sara) knew she (Sara) looked very queer, indeed. All her (Sara) valuable garments had been disposed of, and such as had been left for her (Sara) use she (Sara) was expected to wear so long as she (Sara) could put them on at all. In the evening, when she (Sara) passed houses whose windows were lighted up, she (Sara) used to look into the warm rooms and amuse herself by imagining things about the people she (Sara) saw sitting before the fires or about the tables. It always interested her (Sara) to catch glimpses of rooms before the shutters were closed. There were several families in the square in which Miss Minchin lived, with which she (Minchin) had become quite familiar in a way of her (Minchin) own. The one she (Minchin) liked best she (Minchin) called the Large Family. She (Minchin) called it the Large Family not because the members of it were big--for, indeed, most of them were little--but because there were so many of them. There were eight children in the Large Family, and a stout, rosy mother, and a stout, rosy father, and a stout, rosy grandmother, and any number of servants. The eight children were always either being taken out to walk or to ride in perambulators by comfortable nurses, or they were going to drive with their mamma, or they were flying to the door in the evening to meet their papa and kiss him (papa) and dance around him (papa) and drag off his (papa) overcoat and look in the pockets for packages, or they were crowding about the nursery windows and looking out and pushing each other and laughing--in fact, they were always doing something enjoyable and suited to the tastes of a large family. Sara was quite fond of them, and had given them names out of books--quite romantic names. She (Sara) called them the Montmorencys when she (Sara) did not call them the Large Family. The fat, fair baby with the lace cap was Ethelberta Beauchamp Montmorency; the next baby was Violet Cholmondeley Montmorency; the little boy who could just stagger and who had such round legs was Sydney Cecil Vivian Montmorency; and then came Lilian Evangeline Maud Marion, Rosalind Gladys, Guy Clarence, Veronica Eustacia, and Claude Harold Hector.

One evening a very funny thing happened--though, perhaps, in one sense it was not a funny thing at all.

Several of the Montmorencys were evidently going to a children's party, and just as Sara was about to pass the door they were crossing the pavement to get into the carriage which was waiting for them. Veronica Eustacia and Rosalind Gladys, in white-lace frocks and lovely sashes, had just got in, and Guy Clarence, aged five, was following them. He (Clarence) was such a pretty fellow and had such rosy cheeks and blue eyes, and such a darling little round head covered with curls, that Sara forgot her (Sara) basket and shabby cloak altogether--in fact, forgot everything but that she (Sara) wanted to look at him (Clarence) for a moment. So she (Sara) paused and looked.

It was Christmas time, and the Large Family had been hearing many stories about children who were poor and had no mammas and papas to fill their stockings and take them to the pantomime--children who were, in fact, cold and thinly clad and hungry. In the stories, kind people--sometimes little boys and girls with tender hearts--invariably saw the poor children and gave them money or rich gifts, or took them home to beautiful dinners. Guy Clarence had been affected to tears that very afternoon by the reading of such a story, and he (Clarence) had burned with a desire to find such a poor child and give her (Clarence) a certain sixpence he (Clarence) possessed, and thus provide for her (Clarence) for life. An entire sixpence, he (Clarence) was sure, would mean affluence for evermore. Ram Dass

There were fine sunsets even in the square, sometimes. One could only see parts of them, however, between the chimneys and over the roofs. From the kitchen windows one could not see them at all, and could only guess that they were going on because the bricks looked warm and the air rosy or yellow for a while, or perhaps one saw a blazing glow strike a particular pane of glass somewhere. There was, however, one place from which one could see all the splendor of them: the piles of red or gold clouds in the west; or the purple ones edged with dazzling brightness; or the little fleecy, floating ones, tinged with rose-color and looking like flights of pink doves scurrying across the blue in a great hurry if there was a wind. The place where one could see all this, and seem at the same time to breathe a purer air, was, of course, the attic window. When the square suddenly seemed to begin to glow in an enchanted way and look wonderful in spite of its sooty trees and railings, Sara knew something was going on in the sky; and when it was at all possible to leave the kitchen without being missed or called back, she (Sara) invariably stole away and crept up the flights of stairs, and, climbing on the old table, got her (Sara) head and body as far out of the window as possible. When she (Sara) had accomplished this, she (Sara) always drew a long breath and looked all round her (Sara). It used to seem as if she (Sara) had all the sky and the world to herself. No one else ever looked out of the other attics. Generally the skylights were closed; but even if they were propped open to admit air, no one seemed to come near them. And there Sara would stand, sometimes turning her (Sara) face upward to the blue which seemed so friendly and near--just like a lovely vaulted ceiling--sometimes watching the west and all the wonderful things that happened there: the clouds melting or drifting or waiting softly to be changed pink or crimson or snow-white or purple or pale dove-gray. Sometimes they made islands or great mountains enclosing lakes of deep turquoise-blue, or liquid amber, or chrysoprase-green; sometimes dark headlands jutted into strange, lost seas; sometimes slender strips of wonderful lands joined other wonderful lands together. There were places where it seemed that one could run or climb or stand and wait to see what next was coming--until, perhaps, as it all melted, one could float away. At least it seemed so to Sara, and nothing had ever been quite so beautiful to her (Sara) as the things she (Sara) saw as she (Sara) stood on the table--her (Sara) body half out of the skylight--the sparrows twittering with sunset softness on the slates. The sparrows always seemed to her (Sara) to twitter with a sort of subdued softness just when these marvels were going on.

There was such a sunset as this a few days after the Indian gentleman was brought to his (gentleman) new home; and, as it fortunately happened that the afternoon's work was done in the kitchen and nobody had ordered her (Sara) to go anywhere or perform any task, Sara found it easier than usual to slip away and go upstairs.

She (Sara) mounted her (Sara) table and stood looking out. It was a wonderful moment. There were floods of molten gold covering the west, as if a glorious tide was sweeping over the world. A deep, rich yellow light filled the air; the birds flying across the tops of the houses showed quite black against it.

``It's a Splendid one,'' (Sara/Crewe/Sara Crewe)
said Sara, softly, to herself.
``It makes me feel almost afraid--as if something strange was just going to happen. The Splendid ones always make me feel like that.'' (She)

She (Sara) suddenly turned her (Sara) head because she (Sara) heard a sound a few yards away from her (Sara). It was an odd sound like a queer little squeaky chattering. It came from the window of the next attic. Someone had come to look at the sunset as she (Sara) had. There was a head and a part of a body emerging from the skylight, but it was not the head or body of a little girl or a housemaid; it was the picturesque white-swathed form and dark-faced, gleaming-eyed, white-turbaned head of a native Indian man-servant--

``a Lascar,'' (a man-servant)
Sara said to herself quickly--and the sound she (Sara) had heard came from a small monkey he (monkey) held in his (monkey) arms as if he (monkey) were fond of it, and which was snuggling and chattering against his (monkey) breast.

As Sara looked toward him (monkey) he (monkey) looked toward her (Sara). The first thing she (Sara) thought was that his (monkey) dark face looked sorrowful and homesick. She (Sara) felt absolutely sure he (monkey) had come up to look at the sun, because he (monkey) had seen it so seldom in England that he (monkey) longed for a sight of it. She (Sara) looked at him (monkey) interestedly for a second, and then smiled across the slates. She (Sara) had learned to know how comforting a smile, even from a stranger, may be.

His (stranger) whole expression altered, and he (stranger) showed such gleaming white teeth as he (stranger) smiled back that it was as if a light had been illuminated in his (stranger) dusky face. The friendly look in Sara's eyes was always very effective when people felt tired or dull.

It was perhaps in making his (stranger) salute to her (Sara) that he (stranger) loosened his (stranger) hold on the monkey. He (stranger) was an impish monkey and always ready for adventure, and it is probable that the sight of a little girl excited him (stranger). He (stranger) suddenly broke loose, jumped on to the slates, ran across them chattering, and actually leaped on to Sara's shoulder, and from there down into her (Sara) attic room. The Other Side of the Wall

When one lives in a row of houses, it is interesting to think of the things which are being done and said on the other side of the wall of the very rooms one is living in. Sara was fond of amusing herself by trying to imagine the things hidden by the wall which divided the Select Seminary from the Indian gentleman's house. She (Sara) knew that the schoolroom was next to the Indian gentleman's study, and she (Sara) hoped that the wall was thick so that the noise made sometimes after lesson hours would not disturb him (gentleman).

``I am growing quite fond of him (it),'' (she)
she (Sara) said to Ermengarde;
``I should not like him (it) to be disturbed. I have adopted him (it) for a friend. You can do that with people you never speak to at all. You can just watch them, and think about them and be sorry for them, until they seem almost like relations. I'm quite anxious sometimes when I see the doctor call twice a day.'' (Ermengarde/John/Ermengarde St. John)

``I have very few relations,'' (Ermengarde/John/Ermengarde St. John)

``Who died of a surfeit of lampreys?''' (Sara/Crewe/Sara Crewe)

Sara laughed.

``People you never speak to can't ask you questions like that,'' (she)

``and I'm sure the Indian gentleman wouldn't even if he (gentleman) was quite intimate with you. I am fond of him (gentleman).'' (she)

She (Sara) had become fond of the Large Family because they looked happy; but she (Sara) had become fond of the Indian gentleman because he (gentleman) looked unhappy. He (gentleman) had evidently not fully recovered from some very severe illness. He (gentleman) was not an Indian gentleman really, but an Englishman who had lived in India. He (Englishman) had met with great misfortunes which had for a time so imperilled his (Englishman) whole fortune that he (Englishman) had thought himself ruined and disgraced forever. The shock had been so great that he (Englishman) had almost died of brain fever; and ever since he (Englishman) had been shattered in health, though his (Englishman) fortunes had changed and all his (Englishman) possessions had been restored to him (Englishman). His (Englishman) trouble and peril had been connected with mines.

``And mines with diamonds in 'em!'' (the cook)

13

One of the Populace

The winter was a wretched one. There were days on which Sara tramped through snow when she (Sara) went on her (Sara) errands; there were worse days when the snow melted and combined itself with mud to form slush; there were others when the fog was so thick that the lamps in the street were lighted all day and London looked as it had looked the afternoon, several years ago, when the cab had driven through the thoroughfares with Sara tucked up on its seat, leaning against her (Sara) father's shoulder. On such days the windows of the house of the Large Family always looked delightfully cozy and alluring, and the study in which the Indian gentleman sat glowed with warmth and rich color. But the attic was dismal beyond words. There were no longer sunsets or sunrises to look at, and scarcely ever any stars, it seemed to Sara. The clouds hung low over the skylight and were either gray or mud-color, or dropping heavy rain. At four o'clock in the afternoon, even when there was no special fog, the daylight was at an end. If it was necessary to go to her (Sara) attic for anything, Sara was obliged to light a candle. The women in the kitchen were depressed, and that made them more ill-tempered than ever. Becky was driven like a little slave.

''`Twarn't for you, miss,'' (a slave)
she (Becky) said hoarsely to Sara one night when she (Sara) had crept into the attic--
''`twarn't for you, an' the Bastille, an' bein' the prisoner in the next cell, I should die. That there does seem real now, doesn't it? The missus is more like the head jailer every day she (missus) lives. I can jest see them big keys you say she (missus) carries. The cook she (missus)'s like one of the under-jailers. Tell me some more, please, miss--tell me about the subt`ranean passage we've dug under the walls.'' (she)

``I'll tell you something warmer,'' (Sara/Crewe/Sara Crewe)
shivered Sara.
``Get your coverlet and wrap it round you, and I'll get mine, and we will huddle close together on the bed, and I'll tell you about the tropical forest where the Indian gentleman's monkey used to live. I wonder who caught him (monkey), and if he (monkey) left a family behind who had depended on him (monkey) for coconuts.'' (unknown)

said Becky, gratefully;

Some very odd things happen in this world sometimes.

What Melchisedec Heard and Saw

On this very afternoon, while Sara was out, a strange thing happened in the attic. Only Melchisedec saw and heard it; and he (Heard) was so much alarmed and mystified that he (Heard) scuttled back to his (Heard) hole and hid there, and really quaked and trembled as he (Heard) peeped out furtively and with great caution to watch what was going on.

The attic had been very still all the day after Sara had left it in the early morning. The stillness had only been broken by the pattering of the rain upon the slates and the skylight. Melchisedec had, in fact, found it rather dull; and when the rain ceased to patter and perfect silence reigned, he (Heard) decided to come out and reconnoiter, though experience taught him (Heard) that Sara would not return for some time. He (Heard) had been rambling and sniffing about, and had just found a totally unexpected and unexplained crumb left from his (Heard) last meal, when his (Heard) attention was attracted by a sound on the roof. He (Heard) stopped to listen with a palpitating heart. The sound suggested that something was moving on the roof. It was approaching the skylight; it reached the skylight. The skylight was being mysteriously opened. A dark face peered into the attic; then another face appeared behind it, and both looked in with signs of caution and interest. Two men were outside on the roof, and were making silent preparations to enter through the skylight itself. One was Ram Dass and the other was a young man who was the Indian gentleman's secretary; but of course Melchisedec did not know this. He (secretary) only knew that the men were invading the silence and privacy of the attic; and as the one with the dark face let himself down through the aperture with such lightness and dexterity that he (secretary) did not make the slightest sound, Melchisedec turned tail and fled precipitately back to his (secretary) hole. He (secretary) was frightened to death. He (secretary) had ceased to be timid with Sara, and knew she (Sara) would never throw anything but crumbs, and would never make any sound other than the soft, low, coaxing whistling; but strange men were dangerous things to remain near. He (secretary) lay close and flat near the entrance of his (secretary) home, just managing to peep through the crack with a bright, alarmed eye. How much he (secretary) understood of the talk he (secretary) heard I am not in the least able to say; but, even if he (secretary) had understood it all, he (secretary) would probably have remained greatly mystified.

The secretary, who was light and young, slipped through the skylight as noiselessly as Ram Dass had done; and he (Dass) caught a last glimpse of Melchisedec's vanishing tail.

``Was that a rat?'' (he)
he (Dass) asked Ram Dass in a whisper.

``Yes; a rat, Sahib,'' (Ram Dass)
answered Ram Dass, also whispering.
``There are many in the walls.'' (unknown)

``Ugh!'' (the man)
exclaimed the young man.
``It is a wonder the child is not terrified of them.'' (Ram Dass)

Ram Dass made a gesture with his (Dass) hands. He (Dass) also smiled respectfully. He (Dass) was in this place as the intimate exponent of Sara, though she (Sara) had only spoken to him (Dass) once.

``The child is the little friend of all things, Sahib,'' (he)
he (Dass) answered.
``She (girl) is not as other children. I see her (girl) when she (girl) does not see me. I slip across the slates and look at her (girl) many nights to see that she (girl) is safe. I watch her (girl) from my window when she (girl) does not know I am near. She (girl) stands on the table there and looks out at the sky as if it spoke to her (girl). The sparrows come at her (girl) call. The rat she (girl) has fed and tamed in her (girl) loneliness. The poor slave of the house comes to her (girl) for comfort. There is a little child who comes to her (girl) in secret; there is one older who worships her (girl) and would listen to her (girl) forever if she (girl) might. This I have seen when I have crept across the roof. By the mistress of the house--who is an evil woman--she (woman) is treated like a pariah; but she (woman) has the bearing of a child who is of the blood of kings!'' (unknown)

``You seem to know a great deal about her (woman),'' (the secretary)

``Her (woman) going out I know, and her (woman) coming in; her (woman) sadness and her (woman) poor joys; her (woman) coldness and her (woman) hunger. Then they slipped through the skylight as noiselessly as they had entered it. ``It Is the Child!'' (members)

She (Becky) did not know what would happen to it. It was not likely that it would be left to Miss Minchin. It would be taken away, and the attic would be bare and empty again. Anne

Never had such joy reigned in the nursery of the Large Family. Never had they dreamed of such delights as resulted from an intimate acquaintance with the little-girl-who-was-not-a-beggar. The mere fact of her (Anne) sufferings and adventures made her (Anne) a priceless possession. Everybody wanted to be told over and over again the things which had happened to her (Anne). When one was sitting by a warm fire in a big, glowing room, it was quite delightful to hear how cold it could be in an attic. It must be admitted that the attic was rather delighted in, and that its coldness and bareness quite sank into insignificance when Melchisedec was remembered, and one heard about the sparrows and things one could see if one climbed on the table and stuck one's head and shoulders out of the skylight.
